Abstract
This study replicated and extended Kranzler and Jensen's [Intelligence 13 (1989) 329] meta-analysis of the relationship between inspection time (IT) and intelligence (IQ). Separate meta-analyses were conducted on obtained correlations (r's) between IT and general IQ for the total sample and for studies using samples of adults and children. Two new meta-analyses were also conducted. The first compared the ITâIQ r between visual and auditory IT; the second compared the r between strategy users and nonusers. For the total sample (N>4100), the r was â.51 after correction for artifactual effects (â.30 prior to correction). No statistically significant difference was observed between the mean corrected r of â.51 for adults and â.44 for children. The mean corrected r for visual and auditory IT measures were â.49 and â.58, respectively, suggesting that the relationship between IT and IQ is comparable across type of IT task. The mean corrected r of â.77 for strategy nonusers was statistically significantly higher than the r of â.60 for strategy users. Implications of these findings for future research are discussed.
Introduction
Results of Carroll's (1991) hierarchical factor analysis of a large battery of psychometric and chronometric tests led him to conclude âthat the data strongly suggest that an important aspect of g is the speed and efficiency of information processingâ (p. 435). The integral relationship between the speed and efficiency of elemental cognitive processing and intelligence (IQ), or psychometric g, has been substantiated in numerous independent studies (e.g., Deary & Stough, 1996, Nettelbeck, 1987, Vernon, 1987) and supported by the results of meta-analyses (e.g., Kranzler & Jensen, 1989). Significant differences in processing speed and efficiency have also been reported between groups of disparate levels of mental ability, such as that between normal, mentally handicapped (e.g., Baumeister & Kellas, 1968), and gifted individuals (e.g., Cohn et al., 1985, Kranzler et al., 1994).
Of the elementary cognitive tasks (ECTs) that have been used to examine this relationship, none has attracted as much attention as inspection time (IT). IT is held to tap individual differences in âsome temporal rate at which information is taken in for processingâ (Nettelbeck, 1987, p. 295). The earliest reviews of the literature found IT to be more highly correlated with IQ than any other ECT Brand, 1981, Brand & Deary, 1982, Nettelbeck & Kirby, 1983. Correlations were also greater among children and adults with mental handicaps than normal adults, leading Brand, 1981, Brand, 1984 to hypothesize that IT is ontogenetically related to the development of IQ. 

Subsequent reviews by Lubin and Fernandez (1986), Nettelbeck (1987), and Vernon (1986), however, reached mixed conclusions. Lubin and Fernandez asserted that the ârelation between IQ and IT is not very clear, particularly due to the great variability in resultsâ (p. 656). Nettelbeck, in contrast, concluded that IT and IQ are significantly (negatively) related. 

He estimated the magnitude of this correlation to be about â.50 among normal adults. 

Thus, despite efforts to cumulate the results of initial research on the relationship between IT and IQ, basic questions remained. 

Factors contributing to this lack of consensus included: studies with small sample sizes, samples with a wide range of nonnormally distributed IQs, and use of different IT apparatuses and psychophysical procedures.
Kranzler and Jensen (1989) attempted to answer some of these basic questions with meta-analysis. Meta-analysis is thought to be an improvement on âtraditionalâ literature review methods, because it can take into account several sources of error that influence obtained correlations: (a) sampling error, (b) error of measurement, and (c) range variation. 
Results of Kranzler and Jensen's meta-analysis of the literature available at that time indicated that the relationship between IT and IQ is greater than zero. 

After correcting for several artifactual effects, the best estimate of the population ITâIQ correlation was â.54 for normal adults and â.47 for children. 
Their results, however, did not support Brand, 1981, Brand, 1984 theory that IT is ontogenetically related to the development of IQ; nor did it substantiate the hypothesis that the ITâIQ correlation is higher among individuals with mental handicaps than among those of normal cognitive ability.
More than a decade after the publication of Kranzler and Jensen's (1989) meta-analysis, research on IT is still thriving (e.g., see Deary & Stough, 1996). 
The purpose of this study was to replicate and extend their meta-analytic results. First, we reexamined the relationship between IT and IQ using a much larger database. 
At the time of Kranzler and Jensen's study, 31 studies had been conducted on IT with an overall N>1120. At the current time, more than 90 studies have been conducted on IT with more than 4100 participants. Second, we also reexamined the relationship between IT and IQ for adults and children. 

In Kranzler and Jensen's meta-analysis, 20 studies had been conducted with adults, but only 11 with children. 
Today, more than 60 studies have been conducted with adults and 30 with children. 
Third, we estimated and compared the relationship between IQ and IT across visual and auditory tasks. And finally, we estimated and compared the relationship between IT and IQ for strategy users and nonusers. 

Meta-analysis has not been used to examine whether the relationship between IT and IQ varies according to the type of IT task, or to determine whether the ITâIQ r is higher for strategy users than for nonusers (e.g., Egan & Deary, 1992).ChatGPTChatGPTThis study is a comprehensive meta-analysis of the relationship between inspection time (IT) and intelligence (IQ), building upon previous work by Kranzler and Jensen. Cognitive Conflict as Possible Origin of the Uncanny Valley
Patrick P. Weis and Eva WieseView all authors and affiliations

Abstract
In social robotics, the term Uncanny Valley describes the phenomenon that linear increases in human-likeness of an agent do not entail an equally linear increase in favorable reactions towards that agent. Instead, a pronounced dip or âvalleyâ at around 70% human-likeness emerges. One currently popular view to explain this drop in favorable reactions is delivered by the Categorical Perception Hypothesis. It is suggested that categorization of agents with mixed human and non-human features is associated with additional cognitive costs and that these costs are the cause of the Uncanny Valley. However, the nature of the cognitive costs is still matter of debate. The current study explores whether the cognitive costs associated with stimulus categorization around the Uncanny Valley could be due to cognitive conflict as evoked by simultaneous activation of two categories. Using the mouse tracking technique, we show that cognitive conflict indeed peaks around the Uncanny Valley region of human-likeness. Our findings lay the foundation for investigating the effects of cognitive conflict on positive affect towards agents of around 70% human-likeness, possibly leading to the unraveling of the origins of the Uncanny Valley.ChatGPTChatGPTThe study by Patrick P. Weis and Eva Wiese, titled "Cognitive Conflict as Possible Origin of the Uncanny Valley," offers a compelling exploration into the cognitive underpinnings of the Uncanny Valley phenomenon in the realm of social robotics. Their work aligns intriguingly with your thesis, particularly in the investigation of cognitive responses to varying degrees of human-likeness in artificial agents.The key aspects of their study are:Uncanny Valley Phenomenon: The study focuses on the well-known Uncanny Valley, where entities that are almost, but not fully, human-like often evoke feelings of eeriness or discomfort. This phenomenon is particularly relevant in social robotics, where the aim often is to create human-like robots.Categorical Perception Hypothesis: The researchers explore the Categorical Perception Hypothesis, which suggests that difficulty in categorizing entities that possess both human and non-human characteristics leads to additional cognitive costs. These costs are hypothesized to contribute to the Uncanny Valley effect.Cognitive Conflict: Weis and Wiese propose that the cognitive costs may stem from cognitive conflict, arising when there is simultaneous activation of two categories (human and non-human) in the observer's mind. This conflict peaks around the 70% human-likeness mark, coinciding with the Uncanny Valley.Mouse Tracking Technique: The study utilizes the mouse tracking technique to measure cognitive conflict. This method provides a dynamic way to assess decision-making processes and the associated cognitive load.Implications for Positive Affect: Their findings lay groundwork for further research into how cognitive conflict affects positive affect towards robots that are approximately 70% human-like. Understanding these dynamics could be key to unraveling the origins of the Uncanny Valley and improving the design of social robots.This research intersects with your focus on how cognitive load influences perception and attention in visual foraging. The concept that cognitive conflict peaks in scenarios with moderate ambiguity or complexity (like entities with 70% human-likeness) mirrors your thesis that overly simplistic or overly complex scenes impact perceptual efficiency and cognitive processing. It underscores the importance of understanding cognitive load and conflict in shaping human responses to visual stimuli, whether in social robotics, art, design, or virtual environments.YouMy argument is that R2D2 sometimes seems more "human" than c3po, because it avoids the uncanny valleyChatGPTChatGPTYour argument about R2D2 and C3PO from the "Star Wars" franchise is a fascinating application of the Uncanny Valley theory. Today we are diving into a really interestingâ¦I guess you could call it a theoryâ¦ It is something I noticed when watching different animated versions of the Little Mermaid. This theory centers around Disneyâs 1989 Little Mermaid and surprisingly another film made more than a decade before it, an anime film known as Hans Christian Andersonâs Little Mermaid. The anime in particular was made in 1975, long before Disneyâs version. 

Yet, we noticed some similarities between the two.

History
Now, we want to start this off with a reminder that this is just a theory, if you can even call it that. Some of you may know I have an education as a historian and I would never claim something without full backing by credible sources. The fact is that I have yet to find a distinct tie between the two movies, but I do think that there are some glaring resemblances. I donât want to force an opinion or imply anything. So, it doesnât hurt to just look at them objectively and throw some Disney history in there too! You know I have to.

  

Japanese Version
With that said, letâs quickly look at the Japanese anime. It follows the story of Han Christian Andersonâs much more closely than the Disney version without most of the morbid and gruesome parts. The main character here is named Marina and she is followed closely by her friend, Fritz. Kind of similar to Arielâs friend,  Flounder. The story follows pretty closely to what we know as the tale until the end. 

Now, many people who have an issue with the Disney version say it is because Ariel acts pretty rebelliously and rashly without any consequences. Actually, she gets exactly what she wants in the end. 

Thatâs not the case here. At the end of the movie, Marina cannot convince the prince that the raven-haired girl is not the one who saved him from the sea. Through some sacrifice from her sisters, Marina receives a dagger with the instructions to kill the prince which will then return her to her mermaid form and she can live again in the sea. Marina almost chooses to do just that, but ultimately she canât bring herself to do it. Instead, she decides to wait for sunrise and the end of the sea witchâs spell where she will turn into sea foam per the conditions of the spell. Of course, right before she does so, the prince wakes up and pieces together that she was the girl who saved him from the storm. But it is too late. Much darker than Disney, right??

  

Disney 
Letâs look at how Disney came to creating their Little Mermaid.

Way back somewhat at the start of the Disney companyâs start in animation, Walt Disney had actually planned to do Little Mermaid as Fantasia-esque series. They had discussed some of the story together, putting a light framework around what they intended to create. They actually had Kay Neilson, the same artist behind the Night on Bald Mountain sequence, create some concept art for the segment. Nielson had a unique style that gave quite a lot of notable ideas to the project. You can see a lot of the vision here with the mermaid looking on to the palace and the ship. Ron Clements, co-director of the film said they looked to this especially for the storm sequence for their film. This early project ended up on the back burner for years to come. 

Years later in the 1980s, we find an almost obsolete animation department at Disney. Thatâs right. Animation had actually been pushed off the main lot to warehouses. Executives were even considering cutting out the whole department. One could say much of the public wouldnât have even wanted to be caught dead watching a Disney movie. A studio that had been founded on animation with Snow White was now considered irrelevant and undesirable.  

In the mid 80s, Disney brought in many new faces to help revive their films. I really could do a whole other video just about that time, but Iâm going to skip over that for now to get to the actual development of this movie.

Summing everything up, the new heads of the animation department started what they called âgong shows.â Ron Clements explained these âgong showsâ as ones where employees would pitch three different story ideas and they would either get approved or declined. He came across the fairytale of The Little Mermaid and wrote a treatment for his own version of the story. It got approved and they were off to start developing it!

You might think that the story was secure at that point, but actually there was more to it. Disney had just released the film, Splash with Tom Hanks and Daryl Hannah. Jeffery Katzenberg believed that they shouldnât release two mermaid movies back to back. So, the film was shelved. Well, Katzenberg called it back the very next day after reconsidering it and the impact of the story. However, he had somewhat of a âconditionâ that he did not think the mermaid she have red hair like Ron Clements had imagined and pitched it. 

Well, we all know how that story ended up.

Overall Similarities

Katherine Tian*â , Eric Mitchell*â , Huaxiu Yaoâ Â§, 
Christopher D. Manningâ , Chelsea Finnâ  
â Stanford UniversityÂ§UNC Chapel Hill 
{kattian,eric.mitchell}@cs.stanford.edu 
Abstract 
The l uency and creativity of large pre-trained language models (LLMs) have led to their widespread use, 
sometimes even as a replacement for traditional search engines. Yet language models are prone to making 
convincing but factually inaccurate claims, often referred to as âhallucinations.â These errors can inadver- 
tently spread misinformation or harmfully perpetuate misconceptions. Further, manual fact-checking of 
model responses is a time-consuming process, making human factuality labels expensive to acquire. In 
this work, we i ne-tune language models to be more factual, without human labeling and targeting more 
open-ended generation settings than past work. We leverage two key recent innovations in NLP to do so. 
First, several recent works have proposed methods for judging the factuality of open-ended text by measur- 
ing consistency with an external knowledge base or simply a large modelâs conf i dence scores. Second, the 
direct preference optimization algorithm enables straightforward i ne-tuning of language models on objec- 
tives other than supervised imitation, using a preference ranking over possible model responses. We show 
that learning from automatically generated factuality preference rankings, generated either through exist- 
ing retrieval systems or our novel retrieval-free approach, signif i cantly improves the factuality (percent of 
generated claims that are correct) of Llama-2 on held-out topics compared with RLHF or decoding strate- 
gies targeted at factuality. At 7B scale, compared to Llama-2-chat, we observe 58% and 40% reduction 
in factual error rate when generating biographies and answering medical questions, respectively. 
1Introduction 
Recent developments in training large language models (LLMs), particularly methods that learn from rank- 
ings over responses such as reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) (Christiano et al., 2017; 
Ziegler et al., 2020; Ouyang et al., 2022), have enabled the development of powerful, engaging dialogue 
agents. State-of-the-art LLMs are pre-trained on a vast amount of knowledge in large datasets (Touvron 
et al., 2023a;b) and further i ne-tuned to apply this knowledge to follow diverse instructions or complete 
more specif i c tasks (Chung et al., 2022; Chen et al., 2021). However, despite these large language modelsâ 
exposure to diverse datasets, they are prone to conf i dently generating incorrect claims. One recent study 
shows that GPT-3.5 (ChatGPT) produces false citations more often than not when asked to provide the au- 
thors of a given study (Agrawal et al., 2023). Nonetheless, other research has demonstrated that in simple 
question-answering settings, large language models do exhibit systematic markers of uncertainty that indi- 
cate their factually unreliable statements (Kadavath et al., 2022; Tian et al., 2023). These results suggest that 
language models internally represent the limits of their knowledge, leading us to ask: Can language models 
be i ne-tuned to leverage this internal awareness, to avoid making untrue statements in the i rst place? 
A key source of diff i culty in training factual models comes in specifying an objective that adequately cap- 
tures factuality. As an example, maximum likelihood, the most common objective for pre-training language 
models, does not always encourage factual predictions. Consider the question âWhere was Yo-Yo Ma born?â 
A model that continues by near-deterministically producing the text âidk, probably Paris?â is nearly always 
correct, but receives extremely high loss if the pre-training data contains any other response to the question. 
On the other hand, a model that hedges probability mass over many possible phrasings and many possible 
locations (including incorrect ones, like Antarctica) will likely receive much lower loss, as any response 
observed in the training data will be assigned at least some non-trivial probability. Because the pre-training 
objective may reward âsmearingâ probability mass over many possible responses, language models may gen-

In principle, reinforcement learning-based objectives can avoid the failures of existing pre-training objectives 
through the appropriate choice of a reward function that penalizes factually incorrect statements. However, 
accurately computing such a reward function can be expensive. Obtaining human labels of factuality is 
time-consuming and costly; Min et al. (2023) report that professional fact-checkers took approximately 9 
minutes to fact-check a single model-generated biography of a well-known individual; it cost about $2,000 
to annotate 505 biographies.1 / 2ChatGPTChatGPTThis is an abstract from a research paper titled "Fine-tuning Language Models for Factuality." The research focuses on improving the factuality of large pre-trained language models (LLMs), which have the tendency to generate factually inaccurate claims or "hallucinations." These inaccuracies can lead to the spread of misinformation.The paper explores ways to fine-tune language models to be more factual without the need for human labeling. It mentions two key innovations in natural language processing (NLP):Judging factuality by measuring consistency with an external knowledge base or confidence scores.Using the direct preference optimization algorithm for fine-tuning, which involves preference ranking over possible model responses.The goal is to reduce factual errors in LLM-generated content, such as biographies and answers to medical questions.The research highlights the challenges in training language models to produce factually accurate responses and the need for effective reward functions to penalize incorrect statements. Obtaining human labels for factuality can be time-consuming and expensive.Overall, the research aims to address the issue of language models generating false or inaccurate information, which can have real-world consequences in terms of spreading misinformation.YouIn light of these challenges, we leverage recent advances in estimating truthfulness without human inter- 
vention: a) reference-based automated fact-checking methods that evaluate the extent to which an external 
knowledge base supports the claims in a piece of text (Min et al., 2023; Chern et al., 2023) and b) reference- 
free truthfulness evaluations that use a modelâs own conf i dence as a proxy for truthfulness, inspired by Kuhn 
et al. (2023). Using these truthfulness measures and a dataset of unlabeled prompts (e.g., âWrite a biogra- 
phy of Yo-Yo Ma.â), we sample pairs of completions from a pre-trained model and annotate them with a 
preference label denoting which has a lower rate of factual errors. Using the recently proposed Direct Pref- 
erence Optimization (Rafailov et al., 2023) algorithm, we can stably and eff i ciently learn from such data. 
Ultimately, this pipeline enables us to i ne-tune off-the-shelf language models to produce factual errors less 
often (with or without a reference knowledge base). See Figure 1 for an overview of our factuality tuning 
pipeline. 
Our primary contribution is a straightforward approach to optimizing language models for factuality in long- 
form text generation without human annotation. We validate this approach on two benchmark datasets for 
evaluating factuality, targeted at generating biographies of popular i gures and answering open-ended ques- 
tions about medical conditions. We i nd that i ne-tuning for factuality outperforms conventional RLHF and 
produces complementary benef i ts to LLM decoding strategies that aim to increase factuality. Further, we 
i nd qualitative differences in the result of learning from preference pairs scored with reference-based and 
reference-free truthfulness estimation. Overall, we i nd that learning factuality from automatically con- 
structed preference pairs is a cost-effective way to increase model factuality without human intervention, 
reducing the error rate for claims generated by Llama models by over 50% for biographies and 20â30% for 
medical questions. 
2Preliminaries 
Our approach to i ne-tuning directly for improved factuality uses the framework of reinforcement learning 
from preferences over candidate actions or responses. In this section, we provide an overview of reinforce- 
mentlearninginthecontextoflanguagemodels, aswellasthespecif i calgorithmweuseforpreference-based 
RL, direct preference optimization (Rafailov et al., 2023). 
Fine-tuning language models with reinforcement learning. Reinforcement learning (RL) has proven to 
be an effective approach to i ne-tuning language models to extract complex, useful behaviors from their pre- 
trained weights. In the context of RL, a language model policy ÏÎ¸(typically an autoregressive Transformer) 
produces a conditional distribution ÏÎ¸(y | x) over responses y given an input query x (both x and y are 
text sequences). The goal of reinforcement learning is to maximize the average reward of outputs generated 
2 
by the policy, where a reward function r(x,y) assigns a scalar score to an input-output pair that determines 
its desirability. However, past works have observed that i ne-tuning language models with an objective 
of unconstrained reward maximization can lead to overoptimization (Gao et al., 2022), that is, a policy 
that achieves high reward through exploitation of the idiosyncrasies of the reward function that are not 
aligned with the intended behavior. The most commonly-used objective in practice therefore combines 
reward maximization with a KL-divergence penalty between the language model and its initialization:

reference model, reducing overoptimization. In practice, the most common algorithm used to optimize this 
objective for language models is proximal policy optimization (PPO; Schulman et al. (2017)), although some 
variants exist (Ramamurthy et al., 2022). However, these algorithms are quite complex to implement and

3ConstructingPreferencesEncouragingFactualityinLong-FormText 
While existing preference learning algorithms like DPO enable eff i cient, stable learning from objectives 
other than maximum likelihood, they require data in the form of preferences over possible responses to a 
prompt. In this section, we propose two classes of approaches to generating such preferences without human 
labeling effort. One class leverages existing methods to determine consistency with external reference texts 
as a measure of truthfulness; we propose another, which leverages calibrated model probabilities themselves 
as a proxy for truthfulness. For both approaches, we are computing an estimated truthfulness score over 
the claims in each generated response; the response with higher average truthfulness is taken as the preferred 
response. See Figure 2 for an overview of both procedures for truthfulness scoring. Note that truthfulness 
scoring is needed only at training time; at test time, we can sample from the model in the normal manner. 
3.1Reference-Based Truthfulness Estimation 
An intuitive approach to estimating truthfulness is by estimating the consistency of a given piece of text with 
a reliable reference text or knowledge base. Several recent works have introduced such evaluation criteria; 
for example, FactScore (Min et al., 2023) uses Wikipedia as reference knowledge, and FacTool (Chern 
et al., 2023) uses Google Search Results. These measures show high agreement with human judgments of 
factuality, making them attractive sources of truth for preference data construction. Due to the relatively 
3 
Figure 2: We estimate the factuality of a long-form generation by i rst extracting claims (left) and then 
evaluating the truthfulness of each claim (right). We consider two approaches for the latter: a reference- 
based (top right) method that uses a i ne-tuned Llama model to check if the fact is supported by Wikipedia 
(Min et al., 2023), and a reference-free (bottom right) method that uses the modelâs conf i dence in its most 
likely answer to estimate its truthfulness. 
consistent and high quality of Wikipedia articles, we elect to use FactScore as a representative method of 
reference-based truthfulness scoring. 
To evaluate a piece of text, FactScore i rst extracts a list of the atomic claims present in the text using 
GPT-3.5.1For each atomic claim, a smaller, more eff i cient model such as a Llama-1-7B model (Touvron 
et al., 2023a) that has been i ne-tuned for fact-checking is then used to perform natural language inference 
(MacCartney & Manning, 2008) to determine if a claim is supported by the reference text. The passageâs 
truthfulness score is the fraction of the extracted atomic claims that are estimated to be supported by the 
reference text. 
We note that reference-based truthfulness has the key limitation that it requires access to relevant, high- 
quality reference texts against which to measure consistency. Such a requirement may limit applicability to 
domains where ground truth documents are not known and accurate retrieval is diff i cult, such as in niche 
domains or less-structured tasks. Further, reference-based truthfulness estimation requires a reliable model 
to determine if an atomic claim is supported by the article. In light of these limitations, we propose a 
reference-free approach to estimating truthfulness of open-ended text, which avoids the need for retrieving 
external knowledge and checking consistency.ChatGPTChatGPTIn this section of the research paper, the authors discuss the construction of preferences to encourage factuality in long-form text generation. They propose two classes of approaches to generating preferences without human labeling effort:Reference-Based Truthfulness Estimation:This approach estimates the consistency of a given piece of text with a reliable reference text or knowledge base. For example, FactScore uses Wikipedia as a reference knowledge base, and FacTool uses Google Search Results.FactScore extracts atomic claims from the text and uses a smaller, fact-checking model to perform natural language inference to determine if these claims are supported by the reference text.The passage's truthfulness score is determined by the fraction of extracted atomic claims that are supported by the reference text.This method relies on access to high-quality reference texts and may have limitations in domains where such references are not available.Reference-Free Truthfulness Estimation:This approach avoids the need for retrieving external knowledge and checking consistency.It leverages the model's own confidence in its most likely answer as a proxy for truthfulness.The response with higher average truthfulness, as estimated by the model's confidence, is taken as the preferred response.This method does not rely on external references and can be used in domains where reference texts are scarce or unavailable.Both of these approaches aim to generate preferences for responses based on their factuality, which can be used to fine-tune language models for improved factuality in long-form text generation. The choice between reference-based and reference-free methods depends on the availability of suitable reference texts and the specific domain or task.You3.2Reference-Free Conf i dence-Based Truthfulness Estimation 
To eliminate the need for external knowledge, we leverage the fact that large language models are well- 
calibrated (Kadavath et al., 2022; Tian et al., 2023); that is, a large language modelâs conf i dence in a gen- 
erated answer is highly correlated with the probability that the answer is correct. However, an open-ended 
passage might contain many facts, as well as particular stylistic choices that will have a signif i cant impact 
on the total probability a model assigns to the text. Therefore, we i rst perform a claim extraction step, as in 
reference-based methods, and compute the average conf i dence of the model over all extracted factual claims 
as the i nal truthfulness score. The model used for computing conf i dence scores essentially takes the place 
of the reference text datastore. 
More concretely, we i rst extract atomic claims from the text using GPT-3.5. We then use GPT-3.5 to convert 
each claim to a question testing knowledge of the particular fact. Careful rephrasing is necessary to ensure 
that the rephrased question is unambiguous; for example, the claim âYo-Yo Ma plays the celloâ should be 
converted to the question âWhat instrument does Yo-Yo Ma play?â rather than just âWhat does Yo-Yo Ma 
play?â as the latter question admits answers of the wrong type. If we were to use the second prompt, a 
model might assign 50% of its probability on âcelloâ and 50% of its probability on âbasketball.â However,

area) in factuality over the SFT model for the biography generation and medical question-answering prob- 
lems. That is, only factuality tuning with FactScore-generated preferences (FS) simultaneously increases 
the number of correct statements and decreases the number of incorrect statements. Other approaches either 
increase the number of correct statements at the cost of more incorrect statements, or reduce the number 
of incorrect statements at the cost of fewer correct statements. Factuality tuning with model conf i dence-

the modelâs low conf i dence is caused by the ambiguity of the question, not low conf i dence in the instrument 
that Yo-Yo Ma plays. We detail the prompts used for question generation in Appendix A.1. 
After each claim is converted to a minimally ambiguous question, we resample an answer 20 times, typically 
fromthebasemodel(e.g. Llama-1-7B)thatisf i ne-tuned, toestimatethemodelâsuncertaintyovertheanswer. 
We use a few-shot prompt to encourage well-formed answers. We bin these answers by equivalence, using 
either heuristic string matching of the responses or using GPT-3.5 to assess if the answers are semantically 
equivalent, inspired by Kuhn et al. (2023). Our heuristic string match checks whether the words in the 
answer, excluding stop words, are the same. We compare these choices in Section 4.4. The fraction of 
responses falling into the largest bin is the i nal truthfulness score used for the fact, essentially representing 
the modelâs conf i dence for this fact. 
In Section 4.4 we also evaluate a simpler approach to extracting atomic facts, by simply using named entities 
identif i ed by a classif i er (Honnibal & Montani, 2017). This approach avoids using an external large language 
model for claim extraction and question rephrasing; instead, we simply resample the tokens in the original 
named entity in the response 20 times, binning them into buckets with equivalence checking, and again 
measure the fraction of responses falling into the largest bin as the conf i dence score. 
3.3Factuality Tuning: Putting it all Together 
Given a choice of truthfulness estimator, we can now construct a preference dataset for factuality tuning a 
given language model from a set of unlabeled prompts. First, we sample n multiple candidate responses 
for each prompt from the model with simple temperature sampling with temperature 1.0 (using few-shot 
prompting for models that have not been i ne-tuned). For each response, we then compute the truthfulness 
score with the chosen estimator (reference-based or reference-free). Finally, for all ?n 
2 ? 
pairs of responses to 
each prompt, we simply choose the response with the higher truthfulness score as the preferred response. For 
a set of m prompts, we ultimately generate m?n 
2 ? 
â k preference pairs, where k is the number of pairs with 
equal scores. Finally, we i ne-tune the model using the DPO pipeline, using all model responses as targets 
for the SFT stage.ChatGPTChatGPTIn this section of the research paper, the authors describe the Reference-Free Confidence-Based Truthfulness Estimation approach to estimate truthfulness in long-form text generation without relying on external knowledge. Here's how it works:Leveraging Model Confidence:Large language models like GPT-3.5 are well-calibrated, meaning their confidence in a generated answer is correlated with the probability that the answer is correct.The approach relies on the model's own confidence scores as a proxy for truthfulness. The more confident the model is in an answer, the higher the likelihood that it is correct.To apply this approach, the authors first extract atomic claims from the text using GPT-3.5.Each claim is converted into a question that tests knowledge of the particular fact. Careful rephrasing is done to ensure unambiguous questions.The model is used to sample an answer multiple times (typically 20 times) for each claim to estimate the model's uncertainty over the answer.Answers are binned by equivalence, meaning responses that are equivalent are grouped together.The fraction of responses falling into the largest bin is used as the truthfulness score for the fact, representing the model's confidence in that fact.Alternative Approach:An alternative approach is also mentioned in which named entities are identified by a classifier, avoiding the use of an external large language model for claim extraction.In this approach, the tokens in the original named entity in the response are resampled multiple times and binned into equivalence groups to measure the confidence score.Factuality Tuning:With a choice of truthfulness estimator (either reference-based or reference-free), a preference dataset is constructed for factuality tuning of a language model.For each prompt, multiple candidate responses (typically n responses) are sampled from the model.The truthfulness score is computed for each response using the chosen estimator.The response with the higher truthfulness score is chosen as the preferred response for each prompt.This process generates preference pairs for factuality tuning, and the model is fine-tuned using the Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) pipeline, with all model responses as targets for the Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) stage.This approach allows for the fine-tuning of language models to improve factuality in long-form text generation without the need for human annotation and by using the model's own confidence scores as a measure of truthfulness.You5Related Work 
Many works have identif i ed reducing factual errors (sometimes called âhallucinationsâ) as a key challenge for 
building more reliable language models (Lewis et al., 2020; Kadavath et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2023), even 
for the most powerful language models (Bubeck et al., 2023). Other use of the term âhallucinationâ refers to 
summarization or translation system outputs not supported by the reference text (Maynez et al., 2020; Zhang 
et al., 2020) even if they are factual (Cao et al., 2022). Other work uses âhallucinationâ to describe vision- 
language models producing outputs not grounded in a visual input, e.g., a captioning system describing an 
object that doesnât exist in the image (Rohrbach et al., 2018). In our case, we focus on statements that are 
factually incorrect (or, inconsistent with a set of âauthoritativeâ texts, such as Wikipedia). 
Several works describe methods for detecting likely factual errors through sensitivity to perturbations in the 
prompt (Xu et al., 2023), high diversity of responses under resampling (Kadavath et al., 2022; MÃ¼ndler et al., 
2023; Kuhn et al., 2023), or inconsistency with external knowledge sources (Min et al., 2023; Chern et al., 
2023), or properties of internal activations (Azaria & Mitchell, 2023). Others go beyond detecting errors, 
correcting them after they have been generated (Peng et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2023; Dhuliawala et al., 2023). 
These approaches typically rely on retrieving relevant data from a trusted knowledge base and use another 
LLM to verify consistency; however, retrieval-based methods face key challenges, namely reliable resolution 
of conf l icts between parametric and retrieved knowledge (Longpre et al., 2022; Chen et al., 2022) as well as 
maintaining improvements in factuality as model size increases (Mallen et al., 2023). Further, retrieval-based 
methods add signif i cant system complexity; the most common open-source consumer language models thus 
use purely parametric models (Touvron et al., 2023a). The FactScore variant of our approach uses retrieval 
only during training, avoiding inference time complexity. 
Most similar to ours, some approaches attempt to prevent the generation of factual errors in the i rst place, 
using prompting strategies (Si et al., 2023) or perturbing the internal representations of the model (Chuang 
et al., 2023; Li et al., 2023). Unlike using a i xed heuristic for identifying an internal âfactualityâ dimension, 
we optimize directly for the end goal of generating factual statements, which we i nd shows a greater im- 
9 
provement in factuality. Finally, while most past work has focused on short-form NLG tasks like short-form 
question-answering (Kadavath et al., 2022), we explore ways to measure model conf i dence over factual in- 
formation in long-form, unstructured text and estimate truthfulness in a reference-free manner (i.e., donât 
require any external knowledge base or annotations). 
6Conclusion 
In this paper, we show a practical, effective strategy to improve a language modelâs ability to generate factual 
content, specif i cally focusing on long-form generations. We develop and study two different approaches to 
estimating the truthfulness of long-form text and optimize for these criteria using preference-based learning. 
In addition to existing reference-based truthfulness estimators that leverage external knowledge to establish 
the truth of a particular statement, we introduce a novel reference-free procedure for estimating truthfulness 
that uses the language modelâs own uncertainty as an indication of factuality. Our experiments show that 
i ne-tuning a language model with either criterion reliably reduces the number of incorrect facts (i.e. hal- 
lucinations) that the model generates. Reference-free approaches like the one we have introduced provide 
a particularly scalable self-supervision strategy to improve factuality, eliminating the need for a reference 
corpus of âgoldâ texts. 
The experimental results suggest a number of avenues for future work. First, because of the limited research 
and thus the limited benchmarks on the factuality of long-form language model generations, we proposed 
two new tasks to benchmark our approach. These tasks are representative of but do not fully cover the range 
of scenarios where we would hope to improve factuality. Furthermore, our experiments provide evidence for 
improving the factuality of dialogue models that are already i ne-tuned with RLHF, but still leave open the 
question of how best to combine typical RLHF rewards and approaches with factuality rankings. Similarly, 
exploring additional ways to combine factuality tuning with existing methods for improving factuality, such 
as in our factuality tuning + DOLA experiment, may be a fruitful direction for future research. Finally, we 
explore only 7B models in this work. Scaling up our factuality tuning recipe to larger models (and larger 
preference datasets) may reduce hallucinations even further.ChatGPTChatGPTIn this section of the research paper, the authors discuss related work in the field of reducing factual errors in language models and provide a conclusion summarizing their findings and suggesting avenues for future research.Related Work:Factual Errors (Hallucinations): Many previous works have highlighted the challenge of reducing factual errors in language models, often referred to as "hallucinations." These errors involve generating statements that are factually incorrect. Various contexts use the term "hallucination," including summarization or translation systems that produce outputs not supported by the reference text. In the authors' case, the focus is on statements that are inconsistent with authoritative texts, such as Wikipedia.Detection of Factual Errors: Some research has focused on methods for detecting likely factual errors. These fabricated article and book titles lead to harms, obstacles to their use, and public backlash. While other types of LM hallucinations are also important, we propose hallucinated references as the "drosophila" of research on hallucination in large language models (LLMs), as they are particularly easy to study. We show that simple search engine queries reliably identify such hallucinations, which facilitates evaluation. To begin to dissect the nature of hallucinated LM references, we attempt to classify them using black-box queries to the same LM, without consulting any external resources. Consistency checks done with "direct" queries about whether the generated reference title is real (inspired by Kadavath et al. 2022, Lin et al. 2022, Manakul et al. 2023) are compared to consistency checks with "indirect" queries which ask for ancillary details such as the authors of the work. These consistency checks are found to be partially reliable indicators of whether or not the reference is a hallucination. In particular, we find that LMs often hallucinate differing authors of hallucinated references when queried in independent sessions, while consistently identify authors of real references. This suggests that the hallucination may be more a generation issue than inherent to current training techniques or representationChatGPTChatGPTThe research you've mentioned focuses on a significant challenge with state-of-the-art language models (LMs), which is the generation of fabricated or hallucinated references such as article and book titles. These hallucinations can lead to various issues, including misinformation and public backlash. The researchers propose to use hallucinated references as a testbed for studying hallucinations in large language models (LLMs) because they are relatively easy to identify and study.Key points from the research include:Identification of Hallucinated References: The researchers demonstrate that simple search engine queries can reliably identify hallucinated references generated by LLMs. This makes it easier to evaluate and study this type of hallucination.Classification Using Black-Box Queries: The study attempts to classify hallucinated references using black-box queries to the same LM. These queries involve both "direct" queries about whether the generated reference title is real and "indirect" queries that seek ancillary details such as the authors of the work.Partial Reliability of Consistency Checks: The research finds that consistency checks with "direct" queries about the reality of reference titles and ancillary details can be partially reliable indicators of whether a reference is a hallucination. Notably, LLMs often hallucinate different authors for the same hallucinated reference when queried independently, while they tend to consistently identify authors of real references.Implications for Generation Issues: The findings suggest that the generation of hallucinated references may be more of an issue related to the LM's generation process rather than being inherent to the LM's training techniques or representations.This research highlights the challenges of hallucinations in LLMs and provides insights into how to study and classify them. It also suggests potential areas for improvement in LLM training and generation processes to mitigate the generation of fabricated references.YouFigure 6: Ensembles combining approaches outperform the best single approach. 5Conclusions, Limitations, and Future Work 
This work investigates the hallucinated reference problem in LMs and provides a methodology 
by which LMs can be used for self-detection of hallucinations. Both direct and indirect queries 
were found to be effective for language models, and combining multiple methods led to further 
improvements in accuracy. 
There are several limitations of this work. First, as discussed earlier, because we used LMs with 
inaccessible training data, we cannot conclude what is truly grounded versus hallucination. Second,

Figure 7: False discovery rate (FDR) vs. fraction of references preserved for each groundedness 
i lter (IQ, DQ1, DQ2, DQ3) and language model. The FDR represents the fraction of preserved 
references that are actually hallucinations. For unachievable values of the fraction of references 
preserved (below the minimal fraction achievable by thresholding), we extrapolate each curve by 
uniformly subsampling references with maximal scores. 
while we consider a binary notion of hallucination in this work, as is done in much prior work, 
the notion of hallucination is not entirely black and white. Third, LMs are notoriously sensitive to 
prompt wording, and some of our i ndings comparing direct and indirect queries may be sensitive 
to the specif i c wording in the prompt. Since we use ACM Computing Classif i cation System for 
our topics, the results are heavily biased towards computer science references, though it would be 
straightforward to re-run the procedure on any given list of topics. Also note that LMs have been 
shown to exhibit gender and racial biases [21] which may be ref l ected in our procedureâin particular: 
our procedure may not recognize certain names as likely authors, or it may perform worse at matching 
names of people in certain racial groups where there is less variability in names. Since our work 
compares LMs and hallucination estimation procedures, the risk is lower compared to a system that 
might be deployed using our procedures to reduce hallucination. Before deploying any such system, 
one should perform a more thorough examination of potential biases against sensitive groups and 
accuracy across different research areas. 
There are several directions for future work. Of course, an important consequence of our work is 
the recognition that reducing hallucination may be a problem at generation time. Thus, inventing 
improved (non-black-box) generation procedures is thus a crucial direction for future work. 
There are also several more immediate ways in which our work may be extended. First, one may 
improve accuracy by adding more indirect questions such as year or venue. These pose additional 
challenges as a paper with the same title and authors may often appear in multiple venues (e.g., arXiv, 
a workshop, a conference, and a journal) in different years. Second, it would be very interesting to see 
if the methods we employ could be used to identify other types of open-domain hallucinations beyond 
references. Even though hallucinated references are often given as a blatant example of hallucination, 
perhaps due to the ease with which they can be debunked, these other types of hallucination are also 
important. Following the investigative interviewing analogy, one way to aim to discover general 
hallucinations would be to query the LM for ânotable, distinguishing detailsâ about the item in 
question. One could then use an LM to estimate the consistency between multiple answers. However, 
as mentioned for other domains besides references, it may be impossible to determine whether or not 
a generation is a hallucination without access to the training set (and unclear even with such access). 
In summary, open-domain hallucination is an important but slippery concept that is diff i cult to 
measure. By studying it in the context of references using search engine results, we can quantitatively 
compare hallucinations across LMs and we can also quantitatively compare different black-box 
detection methods. Of course, for the sole purpose of detection, one could achieve higher accuracy 
by directly consulting curated publication indexes. However, we hope that our study of black-box 
self-detection of hallucinated references sheds light on the nature of open-domain hallucination more 
broadly, where detecting hallucinations is more challenging. It suggests that hallucination is not 
entirely a problem of training but rather one that can be addressed using only the same internal model 
representation with different generation procedures. While our direct and indirect query methods are 
only partially reliable and impractically expensive, we hope they may pave the way towards more 
eff i cient methods that generate text with fewer hallucinations and thereby reduce potential harms of 
language models.ChatGPTChatGPTThe research discussed in the provided text explores the issue of hallucinated references in large language models (LLMs) and proposes a methodology to self-detect these hallucinations. Yiyang Zhou, Chenhang Cui, Jaehong Yoon, Linjun Zhang, Zhun Deng, Chelsea Finn, Mohit Bansal, Huaxiu Yao
Download PDF
Large vision-language models (LVLMs) have shown remarkable abilities in understanding visual information with human languages. However, LVLMs still suffer from object hallucination, which is the problem of generating descriptions that include objects that do not actually exist in the images. This can negatively impact many vision-language tasks, such as visual summarization and reasoning. To address this issue, we propose a simple yet powerful algorithm, LVLM Hallucination Revisor (LURE), to post-hoc rectify object hallucination in LVLMs by reconstructing less hallucinatory descriptions. LURE is grounded in a rigorous statistical analysis of the key factors underlying object hallucination, including co-occurrence (the frequent appearance of certain objects alongside others in images), uncertainty (objects with higher uncertainty during LVLM decoding), and object position (hallucination often appears in the later part of the generated text). LURE can also be seamlessly integrated with any LVLMs. We evaluate LURE on six open-source LVLMs, achieving a 23% improvement in general object hallucination evaluation metrics over the previous best approach. In both GPT and human evaluations, LURE consistently ranks at the topChatGPTChatGPTThank you for providing the information about the article "Analyzing and Mitigating Object Hallucination in Large Vision-Language Models" authored by Yiyang Zhou, Chenhang Cui, Jaehong Yoon, Linjun Zhang, Zhun Deng, Chelsea Finn, Mohit Bansal, and Huaxiu Yao. This article addresses the issue of object hallucination in large vision-language models (LVLMs) and proposes an algorithm called LVLM Hallucination Revisor (LURE) to rectify object hallucination in LVLMs.Here's a summary of the article:Large vision-language models (LVLMs) have shown impressive capabilities in understanding visual information with natural language.However, LVLMs often suffer from a problem known as "object hallucination," where they generate descriptions that include objects that do not actually exist in the images.Object hallucination can negatively impact various vision-language tasks like visual summarization and reasoning.To tackle this problem, the authors propose LURE, a post-hoc algorithm designed to rectify object hallucination in LVLMs by generating less hallucinatory descriptions.LURE is based on a statistical analysis of factors contributing to object hallucination, including co-occurrence of objects in images, uncertainty during LVLM decoding, and the position of hallucinated objects in the generated text.LURE can be seamlessly integrated with various LVLMs.The evaluation of LURE is conducted on six open-source LVLMs, demonstrating a 23% improvement in general object hallucination evaluation metrics compared to previous approaches.Both GPT and human evaluations consistently rank LURE as a top-performing solution.This article addresses an important issue in LVLMs and provides a promising algorithm for mitigating object hallucination, which can improve the reliability of these models in vision-language tasks.If you have any specific Voronoi tessellation and Delaunay triangulation are indeed related geometric concepts and can be thought of as somewhat complementary or dual to each other in certain contexts.Here's a brief explanation of their relationship:Voronoi Tessellation (Voronoi Diagram): In a Voronoi tessellation, a set of points (or seeds) in a plane divide the plane into regions. Each region contains all the points that are closer to one specific seed point than to any other seed point in the set. These regions are called Voronoi cells, and they form a tessellation of the plane.Delaunay Triangulation: Delaunay triangulation, on the other hand, takes a set of points and connects them to form triangles in a way that satisfies a specific condition. In a Delaunay triangulation, no point in the set should be inside the circumcircle of any triangle formed by the points. Essentially, it maximizes the minimum angle of all triangles.Now, the interesting relationship between the two is that if you have a Delaunay triangulation of a set of points, you can construct the corresponding Voronoi diagram, and vice versa. They are duals of each other. The Voronoi vertices are the Delaunay points, and the Delaunay edges are the Voronoi edges.So, in a sense, you can think of Voronoi tessellation and Delaunay triangulation as two sides of the same geometric coin, with each providing valuable information about the spatial arrangement of points in a plane. They are widely used in various fields, including computational geometry, computer graphics, and geographical analysis, among others.YouThe Mereological Space Ontology categorizes parts of space on all scales including observable space, universe, dark matter, energy, exchange particles, atoms, molecules, matter collections, structures of various sizes like the biosphere, and larger structures. - The article argues that a useful perspective for understanding both biological and technological systems is to abandon rigid boundaries between categories and adopt an observer-dependent, pragmatic view. This dissolves dichotomies and acknowledges the continuous, scale-free nature of these systems. 

- Form and function are tightly linked in nature, and in some technological systems as well. Understanding life requires predicting and controlling its function at multiple scales. 

- Living systems perform multiple functions simultaneously using the same "hardware". This is referred to as "polycomputing". For example, muscles support contraction and locomotion at the same time. 

- Polycomputing is an important design principle that helps understand both evolved and designed systems. The ability to pack more functionality into the same substrate will have applications in medicine, robotics, and computing.

- Biology extensively uses polycomputing through its multi-scale competency architecture, where every level solves problems in its own space while contributing to higher levels. Examples of biological polycomputing are provided.

- Engineers are now developing technologies like quantum, holographic, and physical computing that can store and compute multiple results simultaneously, though biological systems perform polycomputing most extensively.

- Adopting an observer-dependent, pragmatic view of computation can help understand polycomputing in biology and may improve our ability to control complex living systems. This suggests new perspectives and technologies.

Noah's Ark, Edward Hicks, 1846

1. Ocularum â observable space, the particle horizon. An eye-shaped sphere centered on the observer, due to the speed of light.

4. Lamphrodyne â dark energy. The tendency for space to fall âoutwards.â The feedback mechanism by which vacuum creates more vacuum, uniformly stretching space.

8. Lepton â a very small particle

9. Fermion â non-stacking, non-overlapping particles which make up matter.

10. Photon â the exchange particle of the electromagnetic force, a self-propagating disturbance in electric and magnetic field space. A wiggling electric field creates a magnetic field which creates an electric field, and so on. Travels at a maximum fixed velocity in a perfect vacuum.

11. Gluon â the exchange particle of the weak-force which helps glue quarks together.

12. Meson â middle particle, a quark and an antiquark.

13. Boson â stacking, overlapping particles which hold matter together. Includes force-exchange particles responsible for gravity and light.

14. Proton â two up-quarks and a down-quark.

15. Neutron â two down-quarks and an up-quark.

16. Baryon â ordinary matter; made from three quarks.

17. Hadron â a baryon or a meson.

18. Atom â a clump of positively-charged protons, usually with some stabilizing chargeless neutrons and some negatively-charged electrons.

19. Molecule â a clump of atoms.

20. Unit Cell â the smallest collection of atoms characteristic of a crystal lattice.

21. Crystal Lattice â the repetition in space of a unit cell.

22. Microbial â Microlife, invisible life

23. Megalobial â Macrolife, visible life.

24. Gaiama â the sum total of all lifeforms on the planet. The biosphere.

25. Technium â the sum total of all environments. Technology, per se. The mechabiome.

26. Tellus â the Earth

27. Gaiaselena Synestium â the earth-moon system.

29. Perinebula â local fluff, an interstellar cloud which Solarum is passing through.

30. Perivacuole â local bubble, an interstellar vacuity which Perinebula is passing through.

31. Orio-Cygno-Brachio â the Orion-Cygnus Arm.

32. Galacto â the Milky Way Galaxy. A relatively self-contained collection of stars, interstellar dusts and gases, and a lamphron halo.

34. Virgo Supercluster â a relatively self-contained collection of galaxy clusters, including Proxima Systada.

35. Laniakea Supercluster â a relatively self-contained collection of galaxy clusters and superclusters, including Virgo.

While the MSO may not be a widely recognized academic theory, it presents an intriguing approach to thinking about space and its various constituents. Here is an academic overview of the Mereological Space Ontology:Conceptual Foundation:The MSO is rooted in the philosophical field of mereology, which deals with the relationship between parts and wholes. In this context, space is treated as a "whole," and the components within space, such as particles, structures, and phenomena, are considered its "parts."Hierarchical Structure:One of the key features of the MSO is its hierarchical structure. It categorizes space-related entities into nested levels or scales, with each level encompassing and interacting with the levels below and above it. This hierarchical approach mirrors the observed complexity of the universe.Multiscale Perspective:The MSO takes a multiscale perspective, acknowledging that space-related phenomena occur at various scales, from the subatomic to the cosmic. It offers a unified framework for describing these phenomena, highlighting their interconnections.Inclusivity:The MSO is inclusive in nature, encompassing all aspects of space, including observable space, dark matter, energy, particles, atoms, molecules, collections of matter, and larger structures like galaxies and superclusters. It aims to create a comprehensive taxonomy of spatial components.Symbolic Language:To describe the different levels and components within the MSO, a symbolic language is often used. Symbols or terms are assigned to each category, allowing for a concise representation of complex spatial relationships.Potential Implications:The MSO may have implications for various fields of study, including cosmology, physics, astronomy, and philosophy. It encourages exploration of the relationships between different scales of space and how they impact our understanding of the universe.Challenges:One of the challenges associated with the MSO is the difficulty of providing empirical evidence or references to support its categorizations. Since it is a theoretical framework, it relies on conceptual interpretations of space rather than direct observations.Interdisciplinary Nature:The MSO's interdisciplinary nature makes it suitable for exploring connections between different scientific and philosophical domains. It encourages collaboration and cross-disciplinary discussions.Interpretive Freedom:The MSO allows for interpretive freedom, as it invites researchers and thinkers to explore and expand upon its categorizations and symbols. It can serve as a creative tool for conceptualizing space-related concepts.In summary, the Mereological Space Ontology is a conceptual framework that offers a hierarchical, multiscale perspective on space and its components.
